        <title>Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis | Genome Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis are members of the Solanaceae family that includes tomato, potato, eggplant and pepper. These two Nicotiana species originate from South America and exhibit different alkaloid and diterpenoid production. N. sylvestris is cultivated largely as an ornamental plant and it has been used as a diploid model system for studies of terpenoid production, plastid engineering, and resistance to biotic and abiotic stress. N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis are considered to be modern descendants of the maternal and paternal donors that formed Nicotiana tabacum about 200,000 years ago through interspecific hybridization. Here we report the first genome-wide analysis of these two Nicotiana species. Draft genomes of N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis were assembled to 82.9% and 71.6% of their expected size respectively, with N50 sizes of about 80 kb. The repeat content was 72-75%, with a higher proportion of retrotransposons and copia-like long terminal repeats in N. tomentosiformis. The transcriptome assemblies showed that 44,000-53,000 transcripts were expressed in the roots, leaves or flowers. The key genes involved in terpenoid metabolism, alkaloid metabolism and heavy metal transport showed differential expression in the leaves, roots and flowers of N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis. The reference genomes of N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis represent a significant contribution to the SOL100 initiative because, as members of the Nicotiana genus of Solanaceae, they strengthen the value of the already existing resources by providing additional comparative information, thereby helping to improve our understanding of plant metabolism and evolution."/>

            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );

        function OptanonWrapper() {

                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://genomebiology.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Genome Biology"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis are members of the Solanaceae family that includes tomato, potato, eggplant and pepper. These two Nicotiana species originate from South America and exhibit different alkaloid and diterpenoid production. N. sylvestris is cultivated largely as an ornamental plant and it has been used as a diploid model system for studies of terpenoid production, plastid engineering, and resistance to biotic and abiotic stress. N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis are considered to be modern descendants of the maternal and paternal donors that formed Nicotiana tabacum about 200,000 years ago through interspecific hybridization. Here we report the first genome-wide analysis of these two Nicotiana species. Draft genomes of N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis were assembled to 82.9% and 71.6% of their expected size respectively, with N50 sizes of about 80 kb. The repeat content was 72-75%, with a higher proportion of retrotransposons and copia-like long terminal repeats in N. tomentosiformis. The transcriptome assemblies showed that 44,000-53,000 transcripts were expressed in the roots, leaves or flowers. The key genes involved in terpenoid metabolism, alkaloid metabolism and heavy metal transport showed differential expression in the leaves, roots and flowers of N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis. The reference genomes of N. sylvestris and N. tomentosiformis represent a significant contribution to the SOL100 initiative because, as members of the Nicotiana genus of Solanaceae, they strengthen the value of the already existing resources by providing additional comparative information, thereby helping to improve our understanding of plant metabolism and evolution."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/13059.jpg"/>

                        <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">
                            
    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/"><i data-test="journal-title">Genome Biology</i></a>

                            <b data-test="journal-volume"><span class="u-visually-hidden">volume</span>14</b>, Articlenumber:<span data-test="article-number">R60</span> (<span data-test="article-publication-year">2013</span>)

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p><i>Nicotiana sylvestris</i> and <i>Nicotiana tomentosiformis</i> are members of the Solanaceae family that includes tomato, potato, eggplant and pepper. These two <i>Nicotiana</i> species originate from South America and exhibit different alkaloid and diterpenoid production. <i>N. sylvestris</i> is cultivated largely as an ornamental plant and it has been used as a diploid model system for studies of terpenoid production, plastid engineering, and resistance to biotic and abiotic stress. <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N</i>. <i>tomentosiformis</i> are considered to be modern descendants of the maternal and paternal donors that formed <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i> about 200,000 years ago through interspecific hybridization. Here we report the first genome-wide analysis of these two <i>Nicotiana</i> species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Draft genomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> were assembled to 82.9% and 71.6% of their expected size respectively, with N50 sizes of about 80 kb. The repeat content was 72-75%, with a higher proportion of retrotransposons and copia-like long terminal repeats in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. The transcriptome assemblies showed that 44,000-53,000 transcripts were expressed in the roots, leaves or flowers. The key genes involved in terpenoid metabolism, alkaloid metabolism and heavy metal transport showed differential expression in the leaves, roots and flowers of <i>N</i>. <i>sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The reference genomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> represent a significant contribution to the SOL100 initiative because, as members of the <i>Nicotiana</i> genus of Solanaceae, they strengthen the value of the already existing resources by providing additional comparative information, thereby helping to improve our understanding of plant metabolism and evolution.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Woodland tobacco (<i>Nicotiana sylvestris</i>) grows naturally in the Andes from Bolivia to Argentina and is largely cultivated nowadays as an ornamental plant. <i>Nicotiana tomentosiformis</i> also grows naturally in the Andes but over a wider range, from Peru to Argentina [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Knapp S, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ: Nomenclatural changes and a new sectional classification in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Taxon. 2004, 53: 73-82. 10.2307/4135490." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d53014e742">1</a>]. <i>N. sylvestris</i> (2n = 24) and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (2n = 24) belong to clades of the <i>Nicotiana</i> sections Sylvestres and Tomentosae, respectively, of the Solanaceae family, which have diverged about 15 million years ago [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Renny-Byfield S, Chester M, Kovarik A, Le Comber SC, Grandbastien MA, Deloger M, Nichols RA, Macas J, Novak P, Chase MW, Leitch AR: Next generation sequencing reveals genome downsizing in allotetraploid Nicotiana tabacum, predominantly through the elimination of paternally derived repetitive DNAs. Mol Biol Evol. 2011, 28: 2843-2854. 10.1093/molbev/msr112." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d53014e755">2</a>]. Other members of this family include many agriculturally important species such as tomato, potato, eggplant and pepper. <i>N. sylvestris</i> is considered to be the maternal donor, which about 200,000 years ago merged through interspecific hybridization with <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (most likely paternal donor) to form an allotetraploid <i>N. tabacum</i> (2n = 4x = 48), the common tobacco [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leitch IJ, Hanson L, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ, Leitch AR: The ups and downs of genome size evolution in polyploid species of Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2008, 101: 805-814. 10.1093/aob/mcm326." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d53014e767">3</a>]. Thus, the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome sequences are expected to have high identity to the S-genome and T-genome of <i>N. tabacum</i>, respectively. Both are important for understanding the biological processes - for example, regulation of gene expression, in allotetraploid <i>N. tabacum</i> species.</p><p><i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> are diploid species with an estimated 1C genome size of about 2,650 Mb. As summarized in the Plant DNA C-values database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Plant DNA C-values database. [&#xA;                    http://data.kew.org/cvalues/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d53014e791">4</a>], the genome size estimation based on 1C measurements for <i>N. sylvestris</i> ranges from 2.078 to 2.812 Gb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leitch IJ, Hanson L, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ, Leitch AR: The ups and downs of genome size evolution in polyploid species of Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2008, 101: 805-814. 10.1093/aob/mcm326." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d53014e797">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Galbraith DW, Harkins KR, Maddox JM, Ayres NM, Sharma DP, Firoozabady E: Rapid flow cytometric analysis of the cell cycle in intact plant tissues. Science. 1983, 220: 1049-1051. 10.1126/science.220.4601.1049." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d53014e800">5</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sharma D, Firoozabady E, Ayres N, Galbraith D: Improvement of anther culture in Nicotiana: media, cultural conditions and flow cytometric determination of ploidy levels. Zeitschrift fr Pflanzenphysiologie. 1983, 111: 441-451." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d53014e804">9</a>], with the generally accepted size of 2.636 Gb. For <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, the genome size ranges from 1.809 to 2.763 Gb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leitch IJ, Hanson L, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ, Leitch AR: The ups and downs of genome size evolution in polyploid species of Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2008, 101: 805-814. 10.1093/aob/mcm326." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d53014e810">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Narayan R: Nuclear DNA changes, genome differentiation and evolution in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Plant Systematics Evol. 1987, 157: 161-180. 10.1007/BF00936195." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d53014e813">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Narayan R, Rees H: Nuclear DNA, heterochromatin and phylogeny of Nicotiana amphidiploids. Chromosoma. 1974, 47: 75-83. 10.1007/BF00326273." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d53014e816">8</a>], with the accepted size of 2.682 Gb.</p><p>A subset of simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers derived from the Tobacco Genome Initiative [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bindler G, van der Hoeven R, Gunduz I, Plieske J, Ganal M, Rossi L, Gadani F, Donini P: A microsatellite marker based linkage map of tobacco. Theor Appl Genet. 2007, 114: 341-349." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d53014e822">10</a>] and conserved ortholog set (COSII) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wu F, Mueller LA, Crouzillat D, Petiard V, Tanksley SD: Combining bioinformatics and phylogenetics to identify large sets of single-copy orthologous genes (COSII) for comparative, evolutionary and systematic studies: a test case in the euasterid plant clade. Genetics. 2006, 174: 1407-1420. 10.1534/genetics.106.062455." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d53014e825">11</a>] was used to construct a genetic map for the diploid <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (on a mapping population of <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> TA3385  <i>N. otophora</i> TA3353) and for <i>N. acuminata</i>, a species closely related to <i>N. sylvestris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Wu F, Eannetta NT, Xu Y, Plieske J, Ganal M, Pozzi C, Bakaher N, Tanksley SD: COSII genetic maps of two diploid Nicotiana species provide a detailed picture of synteny with tomato and insights into chromosome evolution in tetraploid N. tabacum. Theor Appl Genet. 2010, 120: 809-827. 10.1007/s00122-009-1206-z." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d53014e844">12</a>]. It was because of the failure to produce a suitable mapping population for <i>N. sylvestris</i> that a mapping population of <i>N. acuminata</i> TA3460  <i>N. acuminata</i> TA3461 was used instead. A high density genetic map of an allotetraploid <i>N. tabacum</i> was built based on a complete set of 2,317 SSR markers applied to an F2 mapping population of Hicks Broadleaf and Red Russian [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bindler G, Plieske J, Bakaher N, Gunduz I, Ivanov N, Van der Hoeven R, Ganal M, Donini P: A high density genetic map of tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum L.) obtained from large scale microsatellite marker development. Theor Appl Genet. 2011, 123: 219-230. 10.1007/s00122-011-1578-8." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d53014e860">13</a>]. Recently, another genetic map of tobacco was constructed from SSR markers applied to a mapping population of two flue-cured tobacco varieties, Honghua Dajinyuan and Hicks Broadleaf [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tong Z, Yang Z, Chen X, Jiao F, Li X, Wu X, Gao Y, Xiao B, Wu W: Large-scale development of microsatellite markers in Nicotiana tabacum and construction of a genetic map of flue-cured tobacco. Plant Breeding. 2012, 131: 674-680. 10.1111/j.1439-0523.2012.01984.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d53014e863">14</a>]. All these genetic markers can serve as anchoring points for validation of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assemblies because of their high similarity to the S- and T-genomes of tobacco.</p><p>In plant biology, <i>N. sylvestris</i> serves as a diploid model system for studies of terpenoid production in glandular trichomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ennajdaoui H, Vachon G, Giacalone C, Besse I, Sallaud C, Herzog M, Tissier A: Trichome specific expression of the tobacco (Nicotiana sylvestris) cembratrien-ol synthase genes is controlled by both activating and repressing cis-regions. Plant Mol Biol. 2010, 73: 673-685. 10.1007/s11103-010-9648-x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d53014e878">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Sallaud C, Giacalone C, Tpfer R, Goepfert S, Bakaher N, Rsti S, Tissier A: Characterization of two genes for the biosynthesis of the labdane diterpene Z-abienol in tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum) glandular trichomes. Plant J. 2012, 72: 1-17. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.05068.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d53014e881">16</a>], engineering of plastid genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Maliga P, Svab Z: Engineering the plastid genome of Nicotiana sylvestris, a diploid model species for plastid genetics. Methods Mol Biol. 2011, 701: 37-50. 10.1007/978-1-61737-957-4_2." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d53014e884">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Thyssen G, Svab Z, Maliga P: Exceptional inheritance of plastids via pollen in Nicotiana sylvestris with no detectable paternal mitochondrial DNA in the progeny. Plant J. 2012, 72: 84-88. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.05057.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d53014e887">18</a>], mitochondrial function [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Pellny TK, Van Aken O, Dutilleul C, Wolff T, Groten K, Bor M, De Paepe R, Reyss A, Van Breusegem F, Noctor G: Mitochondrial respiratory pathways modulate nitrate sensing and nitrogen-dependent regulation of plant architecture in Nicotiana sylvestris. Plant J. 2008, 54: 976-992. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03472.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d53014e891">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Priault P, Fresneau C, Noctor G, De Paepe R, Cornic G, Streb P: The mitochondrial CMSII mutation of Nicotiana sylvestris impairs adjustment of photosynthetic carbon assimilation to higher growth irradiance. J Exp Bot. 2006, 57: 2075-2085. 10.1093/jxb/erj161." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d53014e894">20</a>], herbicide resistance [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Stel'makh O, Kravets E, Emets A, Blium IB: Analysis of reproductive development of mutant Nicotiana sylvestris plants resistant to isopropyl-N-phenylcarbamate]. Tsitol Genet. 2005, 39: 15-" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d53014e897">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Yemets A, Stelmakh O, Blume Y: Effects of the herbicide isopropyl-N-phenyl carbamate on microtubules and MTOCs in lines of Nicotiana sylvestris resistant and sensitive to its action. Cell Biol Int. 2008, 32: 623-629. 10.1016/j.cellbi.2008.01.012." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d53014e900">22</a>] and plant virus resistance [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sekine KT, Tomita R, Takeuchi S, Atsumi G, Saitoh H, Mizumoto H, Kiba A, Yamaoka N, Nishiguchi M, Hikichi Y: Functional differentiation in the leucine-rich repeat domains of closely related plant virus-resistance proteins that recognize common Avr proteins. Mol Plant Microbe Interact. 2012, 25: 1219-1229. 10.1094/MPMI-11-11-0289." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d53014e903">23</a>]. Besides its contribution to tobacco, <i>N. sylvestris</i> has been shown to be a modern descendent of one of the progenitors of other polyploid <i>Nicotiana</i> species (for example, <i>N. nudicaulis, N. repanda, N. nesophila</i> and <i>N. stocktonii</i>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Knapp S, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ: Nomenclatural changes and a new sectional classification in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Taxon. 2004, 53: 73-82. 10.2307/4135490." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d53014e919">1</a>]. Chase <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chase MW, Knapp S, Cox AV, Clarkson JJ, Butsko Y, Joseph J, Savolainen V, Parokonny AS: Molecular systematics, GISH and the origin of hybrid taxa in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2003, 92: 107-127. 10.1093/aob/mcg087." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d53014e925">24</a>] have even suggested that <i>N. sylvestris</i> might have been 'ancestral' to all the <i>Nicotiana</i> species because it easily produces crosses within the genus. <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> has been investigated mostly as a possible ancestor of <i>N. tabacum</i>.</p><p>Similar to other members of the Solanaceae family, <i>N. sylvestris</i> produces a wide range of alkaloids that are toxic to insects; this serves as a well-established mechanism of its natural defense against damage to leaves and flowers caused by herbivores [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Baldwin IT: Inducible nicotine production in native Nicotiana as an example of adaptive phenotypic plasticity. J Chem Ecol. 1999, 25: 3-30. 10.1023/A:1020880931488." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d53014e948">25</a>]. Leaves of the field-grown plants under a defoliation regime exhibit a four-fold increase in total leaf alkaloids depending on leaf position compared with undamaged control plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Baldwin IT: Damage-induced alkaloids in tobacco: pot-bound plants are not inducible. J Chem Ecol. 1988, 14: 1113-1120. 10.1007/BF01019339." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d53014e951">26</a>]. It was shown that wounding induces nicotine transport to the injured tissues via the jasmonate signal transduction pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Baldwin IT: Inducible nicotine production in native Nicotiana as an example of adaptive phenotypic plasticity. J Chem Ecol. 1999, 25: 3-30. 10.1023/A:1020880931488." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d53014e954">25</a>]. Upon wounding, accumulated jasmonates in the shoots are transported to the roots, where they stimulate nicotine synthesis leading to augmented nicotine availability throughout the plant [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Eich E: Solanaceae and Convolvulaceae: Secondary Metabolites: Biosynthesis, Chemotaxonomy, Biological and Economic Significance (A Handbook). 2008, Springer" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d53014e957">27</a>]. Nicotine is the predominant alkaloid in <i>N. sylvestris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Smith C: Occurrence of L-nornicotine in Nicotiana sylvestris. J Economic Entomol. 1937, 30: 724-727." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d53014e964">28</a>] and, unlike for most <i>Nicotiana</i> species in which the roots contain higher quantities of alkaloids compared with the leaves, the total alkaloid content in dry <i>N. sylvestris</i> leaves is the highest (2.96%) in the genus and only 0.786% in roots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Sisson V, Severson R: Alkaloid composition of the Nicotiana species. Beitrge zur Tabakforschung International. 1990, 14: 327-339." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d53014e973">29</a>]. The nicotine content of <i>N. sylvestris</i> (82% of 4.8 mg/g total alkaloids) was found to be much higher than the nicotine content of <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (6% of 0.5 mg/g total alkaloids), and this could be the driving force behind the favorable allotetraploidization between <i>N. sylvestris</i> and other <i>Nicotiana</i> species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Clarkson JJ, Knapp S, Garcia VF, Olmstead RG, Leitch AR, Chase MW: Phylogenetic relationships in Nicotiana (Solanaceae) inferred from multiple plastid DNA regions. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2004, 33: 75-90. 10.1016/j.ympev.2004.05.002." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d53014e989">30</a>]. Nornicotine is the predominant alkaloid (65% of 0.5 mg/g total alkaloids) in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, which is typical for the members of Tomentosae section. In this work, we provide a genomic explanation for the dramatic differences in the alkaloid metabolism between these two <i>Nicotiana</i> species.</p><p>The <i>Nicotiana</i> genus is a rich source of terpenoids, the biosynthesis of which has been reviewed previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Leffingwell J: Basic chemical constituents of tobacco leaf and differences among tobacco types. Tobacco: Production, Chemistry and Technology. 1999, Oxford, UK: Blackwell Sciences, 265-284." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1004">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wagner G: Leaf surface chemistry. Tobacco: Production, Chemistry and Technology. Edited by: Davis DL, Nielsen MT. 1999, Oxford: Blackwell Sciences, 292-303." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1007">32</a>]. Terpenoids play a significant role as attractants to a number of insects that pollinate <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Eich E: Solanaceae and Convolvulaceae: Secondary Metabolites: Biosynthesis, Chemotaxonomy, Biological and Economic Significance (A Handbook). 2008, Springer" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1017">27</a>]. Two of the most abundant diterpenoids, cembranoids and labdanoids, are synthesized in the trichome glands of <i>N. tabacum</i> cultivars. However, <i>N. sylvestris</i> produces predominantly labdanoids and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> produces predominantly cembranoids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Eich E: Solanaceae and Convolvulaceae: Secondary Metabolites: Biosynthesis, Chemotaxonomy, Biological and Economic Significance (A Handbook). 2008, Springer" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1029">27</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Heemann V, Brmmer U, Paulsen C, Seehofer F: Composition of the leaf surface gum of some Nicotiana species and Nicotiana tabacum cultivars. Phytochemistry. 1983, 22: 133-135. 10.1016/S0031-9422(00)80073-4." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1032">33</a>]. To better understand these differences at the genomic level, we attempted to investigate the structure and copy number of the genes responsible for diterpenoid metabolism in their respective genomes.</p><p>Another peculiar property of <i>Nicotiana</i> species is their high susceptibility to accumulate cadmium as well as other heavy metals [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Wagner GJ: Accumulation of cadmium in crop plants and its consequences to human health. Adv Agronomy. 1993, 51: 173-212." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1041">34</a>]. Rosn <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Rosn K, Eriksson J, Vinichuk M: Uptake and translocation of 109Cd and stable Cd within tobacco plants (Nicotiana sylvestris). J Environ Radioact. 2012, 113: 16-20." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1047">35</a>] compared the availability of added and naturally occurring soil cadmium in <i>N. sylvestris</i> plants and found that cadmium concentrations in the leaves was three-fold higher than in the roots, and two-fold higher than in the soil. We examined a set of genes believed to be involved in heavy metal accumulation and describe their structural variations between the two <i>Nicotiana</i> plants.</p><p>High quality genome sequences of tomato and potato have been published and annotated thoroughly by the Solanaceae community [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu X, Pan S, Cheng S, Zhang B, Mu D, Ni P, Zhang G, Yang S, Li R, Wang J: Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011, 475: 189-10.1038/nature10158." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1060">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Sato S, Tabata S, Hirakawa H, Asamizu E, Shirasawa K, Isobe S, Kaneko T, Nakamura Y, Shibata D, Egholm M: The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature. 2012, 485: 635-641. 10.1038/nature11119." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1063">37</a>]. Comparison of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genomes with these two reference genomes will improve our understanding of <i>Nicotiana</i> clade evolution and expedite the functional annotation of their genomes. A draft of the first <i>Nicotiana</i> genome (<i>N. benthamiana</i>, about 3 Gb in size) has recently been published and its utility has been shown immediately in the discovery of homologs of some immunity-associated genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bombarely A, Rosli HG, Vrebalov J, Moffett P, Mueller L, Martin G: A draft genome sequence of Nicotiana benthamiana to enhance molecular plant-microbe biology research. Mol Plant Microbe Interact. 2012, 25: 1523-1530. 10.1094/MPMI-06-12-0148-TA." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1082">38</a>]. Because the allotetraploid genome of <i>N. benthamiana</i> is a result of the hybridization of two <i>Nicotiana</i> species at least one of which is thought to be a member of the Sylvestres clade [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Clarkson JJ, Knapp S, Garcia VF, Olmstead RG, Leitch AR, Chase MW: Phylogenetic relationships in Nicotiana (Solanaceae) inferred from multiple plastid DNA regions. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2004, 33: 75-90. 10.1016/j.ympev.2004.05.002." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1091">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Knapp S, Bohs L, Nee M, Spooner DM: Solanaceae - a model for linking genomics with biodiversity. Comp Funct Genomics. 2004, 5: 285-291. 10.1002/cfg.393." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1095">39</a>], a certain degree of synteny with the genome of <i>N. sylvestris</i> might be expected.</p><p>The estimated genome sizes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (approximately 2,600 Mb) are nearly three times larger than the tomato (900 Mb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Sato S, Tabata S, Hirakawa H, Asamizu E, Shirasawa K, Isobe S, Kaneko T, Nakamura Y, Shibata D, Egholm M: The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature. 2012, 485: 635-641. 10.1038/nature11119." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1110">37</a>] or potato (844 Mb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu X, Pan S, Cheng S, Zhang B, Mu D, Ni P, Zhang G, Yang S, Li R, Wang J: Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011, 475: 189-10.1038/nature10158." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1113">36</a>] genomes, a phenomenon that could be explained by repeat expansion in the <i>Nicotiana</i> genomes due to the accumulation of transposable elements. C<sub>0</sub>t measurements in the <i>N. tabacum</i> genome, which showed the presence of 55% short (approximately 300 nucleotides) and 25% long (approximately 1,500 nucleotides) repeats [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zimmerman JL, Goldberg RB: DNA sequence organization in the genome of Nicotiana tabacum. Chromosoma. 1977, 59: 227-252. 10.1007/BF00292780." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1125">40</a>], support this hypothesis. Similarly, pepper euchromatin doubled its size compared with tomato through a massive gain of a specific group of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Park M, Jo SH, Kwon JK, Park J, Ahn JH, Kim S, Lee YH, Yang TJ, Hur CG, Kang BC: Comparative analysis of pepper and tomato reveals euchromatin expansion of pepper genome caused by differential accumulation of Ty3/Gypsy-like elements. BMC Genomics. 2011, 12: 85-10.1186/1471-2164-12-85." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1128">41</a>]. The <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome showed signs of more recent repeat expansions with higher homogeneity, whereas the genome of <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> showed significantly higher repeat diversity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Renny-Byfield S, Chester M, Kovarik A, Le Comber SC, Grandbastien MA, Deloger M, Nichols RA, Macas J, Novak P, Chase MW, Leitch AR: Next generation sequencing reveals genome downsizing in allotetraploid Nicotiana tabacum, predominantly through the elimination of paternally derived repetitive DNAs. Mol Biol Evol. 2011, 28: 2843-2854. 10.1093/molbev/msr112." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1138">2</a>]. Further, the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome was reported to have a higher content of Tnt1 transposons and a more uniform distribution of the elements than the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Melayah D, Lim KY, Bonnivard E, Chalhoub B, Dorlhac de Borne F, Mhiri C, Leitch AR, Grandbastien MA: Distribution of the Tnt1 retrotransposon family in the amphidiploid tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum) and its wild Nicotiana relatives. Biol J Linn Soci. 2004, 82: 639-649. 10.1111/j.1095-8312.2004.00348.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1147">42</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gazdova B, irok J, Fajkus J, Brzobohat B, Kenton A, Parokonny A, Heslop-Harrison JS, Palme K, Bezdk M: Characterization of a new family of tobacco highly repetitive DNA, GRS, specific for the Nicotiana tomentosiformis genomic component. Chromosome Res. 1995, 3: 245-254. 10.1007/BF00713050." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1150">43</a>]. A more detailed analysis showed that the relative copy numbers of four retrotransposons (Tnt1-OL13, Tnt1-OL16, Tnt2d and Tto1-1R) were higher in <i>N. sylvestris</i> than in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Petit M, Lim KY, Julio E, Poncet C, Dorlhac de Borne F, Kovarik A, Leitch AR, Grandbastien MA, Mhiri C: Differential impact of retrotransposon populations on the genome of allotetraploid tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum). Mol Genet Genomics. 2007, 278: 1-15. 10.1007/s00438-007-0226-0." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1160">44</a>]. Conversely, Renny-Byfield <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Renny-Byfield S, Kovak A, Chester M, Nichols RA, Macas J, Novk P, Leitch AR: Independent, rapid and targeted loss of highly repetitive DNA in natural and synthetic allopolyploids of Nicotiana tabacum. PloS One. 2012, 7: e36963-10.1371/journal.pone.0036963." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1166">45</a>] found that a highly repetitive DNA sequence (NicCL3) made up to 2% of the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome but was almost absent in the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome. A second repetitive DNA sequence, NicCL7/30, was also found to be more represented in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> than <i>N. sylvestris</i>, although not as strongly. Other repeat families, EPRV [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Gregor W, Mette MF, Staginnus C, Matzke MA, Matzke AJM: A distinct endogenous pararetrovirus family in Nicotiana tomentosiformis, a diploid progenitor of polyploid tobacco. Plant Physiol. 2004, 134: 1191-1199. 10.1104/pp.103.031112." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1182">46</a>], TAS49 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Horkov M, Fajkus J: TAS49 a dispersed repetitive sequence isolated from subtelomeric regions of Nicotiana tomentosiformis chromosomes. Genome. 2000, 43: 273-284." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1185">47</a>] and GRS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gazdova B, irok J, Fajkus J, Brzobohat B, Kenton A, Parokonny A, Heslop-Harrison JS, Palme K, Bezdk M: Characterization of a new family of tobacco highly repetitive DNA, GRS, specific for the Nicotiana tomentosiformis genomic component. Chromosome Res. 1995, 3: 245-254. 10.1007/BF00713050." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1188">43</a>], were identified in both genomes and displayed differential copy number, distribution and methylation patterns. These findings emphasize the significant differences in the evolution of the two genomes since they diverged about 15 million years ago.</p><p>The assembly of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> transcriptomes based on 454 sequencing data showed that only 33% of the sequences contained substitutions between the two species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bombarely A, Edwards KD, Sanchez-Tamburrino J, Mueller LA: Deciphering the complex leaf transcriptome of the allotetraploid species Nicotiana tabacum: A phylogenomic perspective. BMC Genomics. 2012, 13: 406-10.1186/1471-2164-13-406." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1200">48</a>]. Bombarely <i>et al.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Bombarely A, Edwards KD, Sanchez-Tamburrino J, Mueller LA: Deciphering the complex leaf transcriptome of the allotetraploid species Nicotiana tabacum: A phylogenomic perspective. BMC Genomics. 2012, 13: 406-10.1186/1471-2164-13-406." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1206">48</a>] suggested that additional Illumina sequencing of the transcriptome should overcome the 'homopolymer' problem due to pyrosequencing and that genomic DNA sequencing would allow an increased number of SNPs to be identified. Elucidating the transcriptomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> can shed light on their protein complement, and allow more targeted experimental investigations of these and related species. Recently an Affymetrix Tobacco Exon Array was developed based on the current genome and EST sequence data from the Tobacco Genome Initiative, which cover a large proportion of the tobacco gene space [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Martin F, Bovet L, Cordier A, Stanke M, Gunduz I, Peitsch MC, Ivanov NV: Design of a Tobacco Exon Array with application to investigate the differential cadmium accumulation property in two tobacco varieties. BMC Genomics. 2012, 13: 674-10.1186/1471-2164-13-674." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1216">49</a>]. Because the probes that cover both the S-genome and T-genome of <i>N. tabacum</i> are very similar to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genomes, respectively; in this study we have used the Tobacco Exon Array to investigate the differential gene expression between the latter two <i>Nicotiana</i> species.</p><p>Here, we present the sequencing and assembly of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> whole genomes as well as the transcriptomes from leaves, roots and flowers. We assess the assembly quality, and analyze and compare them to the existing genomes and transcriptomes from other members of the Solanaceae family. We take a more detailed look at the gene families involved in alkaloid and terpenoid metabolism and heavy metal transport because they should contribute to the unique characteristics of these two plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly</h3><p>The <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genomes were sequenced using a whole-genome shotgun sequencing approach. For <i>N. sylvestris</i>, a 94 coverage of 100 bp Illumina HiSeq-2000 reads was used. In total, six libraries were constructed with different insert sizes ranging from 180 bp to 1 kb for paired-end libraries, and from 3 to 4 kb for mate-pair libraries. The numbers of clean reads in each library are summarized in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM1">1</a>. Similarly, for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> a 146 coverage of 100 bp Illumina HiSeq-2000 reads was used. In total, seven libraries were constructed with different insert sizes ranging from 140 bp to 1 kb for paired-end libraries, and from 3 to 5 kb for mate-pair libraries. The numbers of clean reads in each library are summarized in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM2">2</a>.</p><p>The genomes were assembled by creating contigs from the paired-end reads and then scaffolding them with the mate-pair libraries. In this step, mate-pair information from closely related species was also used. The resulting final assemblies, described in table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Tab1">1</a>, amounted to 2.2 Gb and 1.7 Gb for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, respectively, of which, 92.2% and 97.3% were non-gapped sequences. The <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assemblies contain 174 Mb (7.8%) and 46 Mb (2.7%) undefined bases, respectively. The <i>N. sylvestris</i> assembly contains 253,984 sequences, its N50 length is 79.7 kb, and the longest sequence is 698 kb. The <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assembly is made of 159,649 sequences, its N50 length is 82.6 kb, and the longest sequence is 789.5 kb.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the assembly of the <i>N.</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>With the advent of next-generation sequencing, genome size estimations based on k-mer depth distribution of sequenced reads are becoming possible [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu X, Pan S, Cheng S, Zhang B, Mu D, Ni P, Zhang G, Yang S, Li R, Wang J: Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011, 475: 189-10.1038/nature10158." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1492">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Huang S, Li R, Zhang Z, Li L, Gu X, Fan W, Lucas WJ, Wang X, Xie B, Ni P: The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat Genet. 2009, 41: 1275-1281. 10.1038/ng.475." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1495">50</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Varshney RK, Chen W, Li Y, Bharti AK, Saxena RK, Schlueter JA, Donoghue MT, Azam S, Fan G, Whaley AM, Farmer AD, Sheridan J, Iwata A, Tuteja R, Penmetsa RV, Wu W, Upadhyaya HD, Yang SP, Shah T, Saxena KB, Michael T, McCombie WR, Yang B, Zhang G, Yang H, Wang J, Spillane C, Cook DR, May GD, Xu X, Jackson SA: Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 30: 83-89. 10.1038/nbt.2022." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1498">52</a>]. For instance, the recently published potato genome was estimated to be 844 Mb using a 17-mer distribution [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu X, Pan S, Cheng S, Zhang B, Mu D, Ni P, Zhang G, Yang S, Li R, Wang J: Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011, 475: 189-10.1038/nature10158." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1501">36</a>], in good agreement with its 1C size of 856 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Plant DNA C-values database. [&#xA;                    http://data.kew.org/cvalues/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1504">4</a>]. Furthermore, the analysis of repetitive content in the 727 Mb potato genome assembly and in bacterial artificial chromosomes and fosmid end sequences indicated that much of the unassembled genome sequences were composed of repeats [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu X, Pan S, Cheng S, Zhang B, Mu D, Ni P, Zhang G, Yang S, Li R, Wang J: Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011, 475: 189-10.1038/nature10158." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1508">36</a>]. In <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> the genome sizes were estimated by this method using a 31-mer to be 2.68 Gb and 2.36 Gb, respectively. While the <i>N. sylvestris</i> estimate is in good agreement with the commonly accepted size of its genome based on 1C DNA values, the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> estimate is about 15% smaller than its commonly accepted size. Estimates using a 17-mer were smaller; 2.59 Gb and 2.22 Gb for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, respectively. Using the 31-mer depth distribution, we estimated that our assembly represented 82.9% of the 2.68 Gb <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome and 71.6% of the 2.36 Gb <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome.</p><p>The proportion of contigs that could not be integrated into scaffolds was low; namely, the <i>N. sylvestris</i> assembly contains 59,563 contigs (18 Mb; N50 length of 273 bp) that were not integrated in scaffolds, and the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assembly contains 47,741 contigs (17.3 Mb; N50 length of 346 bp) that were not integrated in scaffolds. Using the regions of the Whole Genome Profiling (WGP) physical map of tobacco [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Sierro N, Van Oeveren J, van Eijk MJ, Martin F, Stormo KE, Peitsch MC, Ivanov NV: Whole genome profiling physical map and ancestral annotation of tobacco Hicks broadleaf. Plant J. 2013, [&#xA;                    http://dx.doi.org/10.1111/tpj.12247&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d53014e1545">53</a>] that are of <i>N. sylvestris</i> or <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> ancestral origin, the assembly scaffolds were superscaffolded and an N50 of 194 kb for <i>N. sylvestris</i> (10,261 contigs and scaffolds in 2,637 superscaffolds) and of 166 kb for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (7,463 contigs and scaffolds in 1,989 superscaffolds) were obtained. Superscaffolding was performed using the WGP physical map contigs as templates and positioning the assembled sequences for which an orientation in the superscaffolds could be determined. This approach discards any anchored sequence of unknown orientation as well as any sequence that spans across several WGP contigs, thereby reducing the number of superscaffolded sequences. Furthermore, the superscaffolding introduced additional unknown bases (N) into the assembly because the length of each stretch was estimated based on the tobacco genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat content</h3><p>The repeat content of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genomes is summarized in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Tab2">2</a>. Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM3">3</a> shows this in more detail. More than 70% of both genomes are repeat elements. In <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, there seem to be more copia-type LTRs (13.43% and 9.13%, respectively) and retrotransposons (13.05% and 10.33%, respectively) than in <i>N. sylvestris</i>; while the amount of gypsy-like LTRs is about 20% in both genomes. The difference between the total size of sequenced DNA and repeat-masked DNA indicates that the gene-rich DNA is around 625 Mb for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and 425 Mb for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Composition of the repeat elements in the <i>N</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>More Tnt1 retrotransposons are found in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> than in <i>N. sylvestris</i>, (7.39% and 3.98% respectively), which apparently contradicts previous reports [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Melayah D, Lim KY, Bonnivard E, Chalhoub B, Dorlhac de Borne F, Mhiri C, Leitch AR, Grandbastien MA: Distribution of the Tnt1 retrotransposon family in the amphidiploid tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum) and its wild Nicotiana relatives. Biol J Linn Soci. 2004, 82: 639-649. 10.1111/j.1095-8312.2004.00348.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d53014e2059">42</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Petit M, Lim KY, Julio E, Poncet C, Dorlhac de Borne F, Kovarik A, Leitch AR, Grandbastien MA, Mhiri C: Differential impact of retrotransposon populations on the genome of allotetraploid tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum). Mol Genet Genomics. 2007, 278: 1-15. 10.1007/s00438-007-0226-0." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d53014e2062">44</a>]. This finding could be caused by the mislabeling of novel <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> repetitive elements obtained by RepeatScout as Tnt1. The amounts of Tnt2 and Tto1 repetitive elements are higher in <i>N. sylvestris</i> than in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> and this finding agrees with previous studies. Furthermore, as reported previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Renny-Byfield S, Kovak A, Chester M, Nichols RA, Macas J, Novk P, Leitch AR: Independent, rapid and targeted loss of highly repetitive DNA in natural and synthetic allopolyploids of Nicotiana tabacum. PloS One. 2012, 7: e36963-10.1371/journal.pone.0036963." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d53014e2075">45</a>], we also observed a higher proportion of NicCL3 (0.75% versus 0.14%) and NicCL7/30 (5.57% versus 2.94%) repetitive DNA elements in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> than in <i>N. sylvestris</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genetic markers</h3><p>The 2,363 tobacco SSR markers reported previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bindler G, Plieske J, Bakaher N, Gunduz I, Ivanov N, Van der Hoeven R, Ganal M, Donini P: A high density genetic map of tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum L.) obtained from large scale microsatellite marker development. Theor Appl Genet. 2011, 123: 219-230. 10.1007/s00122-011-1578-8." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d53014e2092">13</a>] were mapped to both genome assemblies. The number of uniquely mapped markers on each genome was then compared with the results of the PCR amplification tests performed in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, in order to assign an origin to them when creating the tobacco genetic map (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM4">4</a>). Sixty-five percent of the SSR markers that amplified only in <i>N. sylvestris</i> mapped only to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome; 7% mapped to both genomes. Similarly, 65% of the SSR markers that amplified only in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> mapped only to <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>; 15% mapped to both <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. About a third of the tobacco SSR markers could not be mapped. This can be expected, because the current draft genome assemblies are likely to fail assembling in regions with simple repeats such as the ones found in SSR markers. If this is the case, a primer pair will match to two different sequences.</p><p>Of the 173 SSR markers present in the <i>N. acuminata</i> genetic map (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM5">5</a>), 128 (74%) of them could be mapped to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome assembly. This number is the sum of the 75 SSRs of the <i>N. acuminata</i> map found in the <i>N. sylvestris</i> assembly, the 50 SSRs of the <i>N. acuminata</i> map found in the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assemblies, the single SSR of the <i>N. acuminata</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> maps found in the <i>N. sylvestris</i> assembly, and the 2 SSRs of the <i>N. acuminata</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> maps found in the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assemblies (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM6">6</a>). Similarly, of the 221 SSR markers present in the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genetic map (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM7">7</a>), 173 (78%) could be mapped to the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM6">6</a>). In addition, 706 SSR markers not present on the existing genetic maps could be mapped to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome assembly, 605 mapped to the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assembly, and 174 mapped to both.</p><p>Of the 134 COSII markers present in the <i>N. acuminata</i> genetic map, 45 (34%) could be mapped to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM8">8</a>). Similarly, of the 262 COSII markers in the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genetic map, 81 (31%) could be mapped to the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assembly (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM8">8</a>). Using the same method, 736 of the 879 COSII markers (84%) on the expen2000 tomato genetic map could be found; 718 of them mapped to the expected chromosome. In addition, 68 COSII markers not present on the existing genetic maps could be mapped to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome assembly, 78 mapped to the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assembly, and 226 mapped to both.</p><p>The low numbers of COSII markers that could be mapped to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assemblies, despite the good results that were obtained using the same method on the tomato map, could be due to the current fragmented state of the assemblies, or because the COSII marker primers are not adapted for <i>Nicotiana</i> species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Transcriptome assembly</h3><p>The number of reads obtained for each of the tissue-specific samples from both species is outlined in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM9">9</a>. Tissue-specific assemblies were generated for the three samples (root, leaf and flower) by mapping the reads to the reference genomes using the Bowtie2/Tophat2 pipeline. The length distributions of the assembled transcripts are summarized in table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Tab3">3</a>. In addition, a reference transcriptome for each species was created by merging the three individual tissue-specific assemblies. We also used a <i>de novo</i> assembly program to generate an assembly that potentially contains transcripts missing from the mapping assembly because of the absence of certain genes from the current reference genome assembly. The size and length distribution of the assembled transcripts is shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM10">10</a>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Number and length distribution of transcripts from the tissue-specific read mapping using Cufflinks2</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Transcript and protein quality</h3><p>The assembled reference transcriptome was assessed for completeness and accuracy by mapping the transcripts to the UniProt reference plant sequence databases. The number of sequences for both the transcripts and the unique genes from which the transcripts are derived that could be mapped was similar for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Fig1">1</a>). For <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, 58.6% and 60.5% of transcripts, respectively, had significant ORFs with a length equal to or longer than 100 amino acids. The majority, 82.2% for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and 81.9% for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, had a homologous sequence in the UniProt Knowledgebase. Approximately a third of these peptide sequences, 37.2% in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and 36.5% in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, had hits in Swiss-Prot, the annotated subset of UniProt. The BLAST alignments show that while the coverage of the predicted ORFs by the reference sequences is generally high (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Fig2">2</a>) and comparable between the species, the coverage of the reference sequence by the predicted ORFs is often partial, indicating that these ORFs are likely to be incomplete.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div
