Histograms for the coverage of the query and reference show the categorized number of BLAST hits.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Functional comparison to other species</h3><p>We used the OrthoMCL software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Chen F, Mackey AJ, Vermunt JK, Roos DS: Assessing performance of orthology detection strategies applied to eukaryotic genomes. PLoS One. 2007, 2: e383-10.1371/journal.pone.0000383." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d53014e2718">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ, Roos DS: OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003, 13: 2178-2189. 10.1101/gr.1224503." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d53014e2721">55</a>] to define clusters of orthologous and paralogous genes between <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, as well as tomato, another representative of the Solanaceae family, and <i>Arabidopsis</i> as a representative of the eudicots (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Fig3">3</a>). While a large number of sequences are shared between all the species (7,098), many are specific to Solanaceae (2,790). A very high number of sequences are only observed in the <i>Nicotiana</i> species (3,614), with several hundred gene clusters being specific to <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. These sequences may be artifacts that are the result of incomplete transcripts not clustering correctly, rather than actual novel protein families that evolved since the split of the species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>Clusters of orthologous genes from</b> <b><i>N</i></b>. <i>sylvestris</i>, <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, tomato and <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The genes for the predicted <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> ORFs are from the merged transcript assemblies. Tomato is a representative of Solanaceae and <i>Arabidopsis</i> is a representative eudicot.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>At the tissue level (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Fig4">4</a>), the vast majority of gene clusters are shared. As far as the number of clusters is concerned, flowers had the most diverse transcriptome; flowers also contain a large number of transcripts (3,510 for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and 3,387 for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>) not found in root or leaf tissues. The number of tissue-specific clusters is very low (&lt;20 for any of the tissues in either of the species); this number reflects the 'noise level' of the merging process because in choosing representative transcripts while merging of the tissue transcriptomes, a different set of exons may have been chosen, and the tissue sequences may not match the representative in the merged transcriptome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>Clusters of orthologous genes from the individual tissue and merged sample reads</b>. The gene sequences are derived from RNA-seq reads. <b>(a)</b> The gene clusters for <i>N. sylvestris</i>. <b>(b)</b> The gene clusters for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Functional annotation</h3><p>Function assignment for proteins was performed by computational means, using the EFICAz program to assign Enzyme Commission (EC) numbers and the InterProScan software to assign Gene Ontology (GO) terms (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Tab4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Functional annotation of the predicted proteome from the <i>N.</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Over 7,000 proteins could be annotated with a three-digit EC number using the EFICAz tool (7,554 in <i>N. sylvestris</i>, 7,397 in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>), of which over 4,000 were assigned with high confidence (4,061 in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and 4,008 in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>). This implies that just less than 20% of the predicted proteome of the two species (19.4% and 19.1% for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>) has enzymatic function. Just over 4,000 and over 3,000 four-digit EC numbers could be assigned to predicted proteins. Although the number of unique four-digit EC numbers is comparatively small (635 for both <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>), this information can still be used to generate molecular pathway databases.</p><p>Approximately half of all the proteins were annotated with at least one GO term by the InterProScan software (57.8% for <i>N. sylvestris</i> and 57.4% for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>); close to 50,000 biological process tags were assigned and slightly more than 20,000 molecular functions were assigned to just under 20,000 unique proteins. GO term enrichment was analyzed using the GOStats package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Falcon S, Gentleman R: Using GOstats to test gene lists for GO term association. Bioinformatics. 2007, 23: 257-258. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btl567." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3278">56</a>]. Enrichment was tested by comparing the GO term complement for each species against the background of the pooled set of GO terms from both organisms. We see only small and not highly significant changes in gene composition. For <i>N. sylvestris</i>, the defense response function is overrepresented; in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> we observe an enrichment of core metabolic functions as well as protein phosphorylation. The phenotypic differences between the species are thus likely to be regulatory rather than due to the loss or gains of new genes. A caveat exists in that the absence of a gene in the genome of one species does not guarantee that the gene does not exist: it is possible that the section of the genome containing the gene simply has not been covered by our current efforts and that further sequencing will identify these. The results of GO term enrichment analysis are shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM11">11</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Heavy-metal transport</h3><p>A non-exhaustive list of gene copies that may be involved in cadmium/zinc (Cd/Zn) accumulation (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Fig5">5</a>) in <i>Nicotiana</i> leaves is shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM12">12</a>. The corresponding transcripts in root, leaf and flower are depicted. The expression data resulting from the hybridization of specific Affymetrix probes (100% matches with the targeted sequences) with leaf RNA isolated from <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> provided data similar to fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (FPKM) expression data. The results show that the design of the Affymetrix exon probes is suitable for the analyses of gene expression in both tobacco ancestors, <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>Diagrammatic representation of heavy-metal transport and accumulation in</b> <b><i>Nicotiana</i></b> <b>leaves</b>. Left circle: cadmium is transported by IRT from the environment in the root, and then by HMA into the phloem. Middle: NA and PCS transport cadmium from the root to the leaves. Right circle: in roots and leaves, cadmium enters cells, where it is transported to the vacuole by HMA, ABC or CAX. ABC also exports cadmium outside of the cell. ABC, ATP-binding cassette transporter; CAX, cation/proton exchanger; HMA, heavy metal ATPase; IRT, iron transporter protein; NA, nicotinamine synthase; PCS, phytochelatin synthase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Based on sequence and expression data analogies with corresponding <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> gene data, two <i>Nicotiana</i> iron transport-related sequences belonging to the IRT family were identified and named NsylIRT1, NtomIRT1 and NsylIRT2, NtomIRT2 corresponding to <i>Arabidopsis</i> IRT1 and IRT2. Both of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genes are expressed in the roots and are involved in Zn/Cd uptake [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Guerinot ML: The ZIP family of metal transporters. Biochim Biophys Acta. 2000, 1465: 190-198. 10.1016/S0005-2736(00)00138-3." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3362">57</a>], although IRT1 is more selective for iron [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Varotto C, Maiwald D, Pesaresi P, Jahns P, Salamini F, Leister D: The metal ion transporter IRT1 is necessary for iron homeostasis and efficient photosynthesis in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant J. 2002, 31: 589-599. 10.1046/j.1365-313X.2002.01381.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3366">58</a>]. Interestingly<i>, IRT1</i> and <i>IRT2</i> are expressed in <i>N. sylvestris</i> roots but not in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> roots, suggesting that one or more other genes, possibly belonging to the ZIP family, function for Zn and iron uptake in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kim SA, Guerinot ML: Mining iron: iron uptake and transport in plants. FEBS Lett. 2007, 581: 2273-2280. 10.1016/j.febslet.2007.04.043." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3385">59</a>]. Conversely, the potential <i>Nicotiana</i> orthologs (<i>NtomIRT3</i> and <i>NsylIRT3</i>) of <i>AtIRT3</i> are not expressed in the roots (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM12">12</a>), although <i>AtIRT3</i> is expressed in <i>Arabidopsis</i> roots, where it is involved in Zn and iron transport [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Lin YF, Liang HM, Yang SY, Boch A, Clemens S, Chen CC, Wu JF, Huang JL, Yeh KC: Arabidopsis IRT3 is a zinc-regulated and plasma membrane localized zinc/iron transporter. New Phytol. 2009, 182: 392-404. 10.1111/j.1469-8137.2009.02766.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3410">60</a>]. Interestingly, <i>NsylIRT3</i> and <i>NtomIRT3</i> transcripts are more abundant in flower tissues most likely for the redistribution of Zn and Fe. The function of <i>Nicotiana</i> <i>IRT3</i> is possibly closer to the Zrt/IRT-like protein <i>AtZIP4</i>, which is highly expressed in anther and pollen [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="GENEVESTIGATOR plant biology. [&#xA;                    https://www.genevestigator.com/gv/plant.jsp&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3429">61</a>], where it is suspected to play a role in Zn redistribution in flowers ([<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Gainza-Corts F, Prez-Daz R, Prez-Castro R, Tapia J, Casaretto JA, Gonzlez S, Pea-Corts H, Ruiz-Lara S, Gonzlez E: Characterization of a putative grapevine Zn transporter, VvZIP3, suggests its involvement in early reproductive development in Vitis vinifera L. BMC Plant Biol. 2012, 12: 111-10.1186/1471-2229-12-111." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3432">62</a>] and references therein). Thus, Zn and iron uptake is likely driven by AtIRT1 and AtIRT2 orthologous proteins in <i>N. sylvestris</i>, whereas another gene(s) is likely to perform this function in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>.</p><p>The P<sub>1B</sub>-type ATPases, known as heavy metal ATPases (HMAs), play important roles in metal transport in plants. In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, AtHMA3 (OsHMA3 in rice) is localized in the tonoplast membrane, where it plays a major role in detoxifying Zn and Cd through vacuolar sequestration. AtHMA3 is recognized as the major locus responsible for the variation in leaf Cd accumulation of <i>A. thaliana</i> accessions. AtHMA2 (OsHMA2 in rice) and AtHMA4 (likely OsHMA9 in rice), are localized in the plasma membrane and are expressed in the tissues that surround the vascular vessels of roots, where they function in Zn and Cd efflux from cells [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Takahashi R, Bashir K, Ishimaru Y, Nishizawa NK, Nakanishi H: The role of heavy-metal ATPases, HMAs, in zinc and cadmium transport in rice. Plant Signal Behav. 2012, 7: 0-1." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3452">63</a>]. In <i>N. sylvestris</i>, <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (Solyc07g009130) genomes, only one <i>HMA</i> gene orthologous to the sub-cluster formed by <i>AtHMA2</i>, <i>AtHMA3</i> and <i>AtHMA4</i> in <i>A. thaliana</i> is present. This suggests a strong evolutionary divergence between Brassicaceae-Poaceae and Solanaceae. The FPKM expression data show major expression of <i>Nicotiana</i> HMA in the root tissues, suggesting that it has functions that are similar to those of AtHMA2, AtHMA3 and AtHMA4, and is more involved in Zn/Co/Cd/Pb translocation from root to shoot than in vacuolar sequestration.</p><p>The long-distance root-to-shoot transport of Cd/Zn can be driven by phytochelatins or nicotianamine. Therefore, the key genes that may affect Cd/Zn accumulation in leaves are phytochelatin synthases (PCS) and nicotianamine synthetases (NS) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Gong JM, Lee DA, Schroeder JI: Long-distance root-to-shoot transport of phytochelatins and cadmium in Arabidopsis. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA. 2003, 100: 10118-10123. 10.1073/pnas.1734072100." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3487">64</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Deinlein U, Weber M, Schmidt H, Rensch S, Trampczynska A, Hansen TH, Husted S, Schjoerring JK, Talke IN, Krmer U: Elevated nicotianamine levels in Arabidopsis halleri roots play a key role in zinc hyperaccumulation. Plant Cell Online. 2012, 24: 708-723. 10.1105/tpc.111.095000." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3490">65</a>]. The orthologous genes (<i>PCS</i>, <i>NS1</i> and <i>NS2</i>) identified in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> exhibit similar expression profiles in the root, leaf and flower tissues, suggesting that transport in vascular tissues is similar in both <i>Nicotiana</i> species.</p><p>Genes orthologous to the ABC transporters that are involved in Cd transport in <i>A. thaliana</i>, such as <i>AtPDR8</i> (PDR) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Kim DY, Bovet L, Maeshima M, Martinoia E, Lee Y: The ABC transporter AtPDR8 is a cadmium extrusion pump conferring heavy metal resistance. Plant J. 2007, 50: 207-218. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2007.03044.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3522">66</a>] and <i>AtATM3</i> (ATM) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kim DY, Bovet L, Kushnir S, Noh EW, Martinoia E, Lee Y: AtATM3 is involved in heavy metal resistance in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 2006, 140: 922-932. 10.1104/pp.105.074146." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3528">67</a>], are found in both the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome. Their expression profiles are similar in both <i>Nicotiana</i> species and close to their expression profiles in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, suggesting that these genes have similar functions in root, leaf and flower in both species. ABC proteins related to the multidrug resistance-associated protein (MRP) family have been already described to be involved in Cd transport and sequestration [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Bovet L, Feller U, Martinoia E: Possible involvement of plant ABC transporters in cadmium detoxification: a cDNA sub-microarray approach. Environ Int. 2005, 31: 263-267. 10.1016/j.envint.2004.10.011." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3544">68</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Gaillard S, Jacquet H, Vavasseur A, Leonhardt N, Forestier C: AtMRP6/AtABCC6, an ATP-Binding Cassette transporter gene expressed during early steps of seedling development and up-regulated by cadmium in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Plant Biol. 2008, 8: 22-10.1186/1471-2229-8-22." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3547">69</a>] Although the precise cellular function of one of the MRP family members in <i>N. tabacum</i>, NtMRP4, has not yet been determined, silencing <i>NtMRP4</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Bovet L: Heavy Metal Reduction in Planta. 2012, WO Patent WO/2012/028,309" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3557">70</a>] resulted in Cd reduction in leaves under field conditions. The corresponding gene is expressed in both <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, suggesting that it has similar functions in both plants.</p><p>Other genes that play a role in Cd accumulation into vacuoles belong to the cation proton exchanger (CAX) family. Overexpression of <i>AtCAX2</i> and <i>AtCAX4</i> in tobacco resulted in Cd accumulation in the roots and a subsequent decrease in the shoots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Korenkov V, King B, Hirschi K, Wagner GJ: Root-selective expression of AtCAX4 and AtCAX2 results in reduced lamina cadmium in field-grown Nicotiana tabacum L. Plant Biotechnol J. 2009, 7: 219-226. 10.1111/j.1467-7652.2008.00390.x." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3575">71</a>]. Four genes that clustered with <i>AtCAX2</i> and not <i>AtCAX4</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM13">13</a>) were identified in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, suggesting that tobacco CAX gene products orthologous to AtCAX2 and not AtCAX4 may play roles in Cd sequestration in <i>Nicotiana</i> species. The expression profiles of the four genes are similar in both <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, indicating that these genes play identical functions in both plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Alkaloid metabolism</h3><p>The key genes involved in the synthesis of nicotine and nornicotine alkaloids in <i>Nicotiana</i> leaves (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#Fig6">6</a>) are listed in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM14">14</a> and the corresponding transcripts in root, leaf and flower are shown. The expression data obtained from the hybridization of specific Affymetrix probes (100% match with the targeted sequences) with leaf RNA isolated from <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> provided data similar to FPKM expression, except for four <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genes - <i>NtomQPT1</i>, <i>NtomBBL3</i>, <i>NtomNND1</i> and <i>NtomNND2</i>. However, these four genes were found to be expressed in the leaf of <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> plants subjected to RNA-seq analyses. The plants that were used for the RNA-seq analyses were fully mature compared with the young plantlets that were used for the Tobacco Exon Array hybridization, which may indicate that the four genes are more highly expressed in mature leaves than in the primary leaves, suggesting that these genes may possibly affect the alkaloid pathway. Similar to the Cd genes described above, this type of comparison confirms that the design of the Affymetrix exon probes is suitable for the analyses of gene expression in both <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-6-r60/MediaObjects/13059_2012_3094_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>Key genes involved in the synthesis of nicotine and nornicotine alkaloids in</b> <b><i>Nicotiana</i></b> <b>leaves</b>. The berberine bridge enzyme-like protein converts nicotinic acid and <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolinium cation into nicotine, and nicotine <i>N</i>-deaminase converts it further to nornicotine. AO, L-aspartate oxidase; BBL, berberine bridge enzyme-like protein; MPO, methyleputrescine oxidase; NND, nicotine N-demethylase; PMT, putrecine N-methyltransferase; QPT, quinolinate phosphoribosyltransferase; QS, quinolinate synthase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The higher accumulation of nicotine in <i>N. sylvestris</i> compared with <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> is because of the relatively large deletion that encompasses the NIC2 locus of <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. Therefore, the low-nicotine phenotype is often associated with <i>nic2</i> mutations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Shoji T, Kajikawa M, Hashimoto T: Clustered transcription factor genes regulate nicotine biosynthesis in tobacco. Plant Cell Online. 2010, 22: 3390-3409. 10.1105/tpc.110.078543." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3699">72</a>]. In <i>nic1nic2</i> mutant roots, BBL transcripts are strongly reduced [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Kajikawa M, Shoji T, Kato A, Hashimoto T: Vacuole-localized berberine bridge enzyme-like proteins are required for a late step of nicotine biosynthesis in tobacco. Plant Physiol. 2011, 155: 2010-2022. 10.1104/pp.110.170878." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3706">73</a>], attesting that berberine bridge enzyme-like (BBL) genes are regulated by the NIC loci in the roots. Our data confirm that <i>BBL1</i> and <i>BBL3</i> are particularly expressed in the roots of both <i>Nicotiana</i> species. However, no large differences in transcript levels were found, possibly suggesting that BBL gene regulation is not as different as suspected between <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, and the effect of the <i>nic2</i> deletion is apparent somewhere else within the nicotine biosynthesis pathway. In this context, our data show that the expression of a large set of genes involved in nicotine biosynthesis, for example, L-aspartate oxidase (AO), quinolinate synthase (QS), quinolinate phosphoribosyltransferase (QPT), and putrecine N-methyltransferase (PMT), are strongly up-regulated in the roots of <i>N. sylvestris</i> compared with <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>; indeed, <i>PMT</i> expression is not detected in the roots of <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. Four different <i>PMT</i> genes have been found in <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Biastoff S, Brandt W, Drger B: Putrescine N-methyltransferase - The start for alkaloids. Phytochemistry. 2009, 70: 1708-1718. 10.1016/j.phytochem.2009.06.012." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3747">74</a>] and, based on sequence analogy, three of them [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Shoji T, Yamada Y, Hashimoto T: Jasmonate induction of putrescine N-methyltransferase genes in the root of Nicotiana sylvestris. Plant Cell Physiol. 2000, 41: 831-839. 10.1093/pcp/pcd001." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3750">75</a>] likely originate from <i>N. sylvestris</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM15">15</a>). Surprisingly, the two copies of <i>PMT</i> that are present in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> are similar to only one <i>PMT</i> gene in <i>N. tabacum</i> (<i>NtPMT-2</i>). This finding suggests that because of the lack of the three other PMT copies in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, the full pathway for nicotine synthesis is certainly different in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> than in <i>N. sylvestris</i>, which has three PMT copies that are related to <i>N. tabacum</i>, NtPMT-1, -3 and -4 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM15">15</a>).</p><p>The up-regulation of <i>PMTs</i>, <i>AO</i> and QS in <i>N. sylvestris</i> compared with <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> attests that the early steps in the pathway that lead to the synthesis of nicotinic acid are also particularly active in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and certainly play a major role in nicotine synthesis. Recent data reported by Shoji and Hashimoto [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Shoji T, Hashimoto T: Tobacco MYC2 regulates jasmonate-inducible nicotine biosynthesis genes directly and by way of the NIC2-locus ERF genes. Plant Cell Physiol. 2011, 52: 1117-1130. 10.1093/pcp/pcr063." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3810">76</a>] suggest that tobacco MYC2 regulates PMT-2 and QPT-2 by interacting with specific promoter regions. It is therefore tempting to speculate that regulation occurs differently via MYC2 in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. Conversely, because AO and QS are located in the plastids and are involved in NAD synthesis from aspartate via quinolinic acid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Katoh A, Uenohara K, Akita M, Hashimoto T: Early steps in the biosynthesis of NAD in Arabidopsis start with aspartate and occur in the plastid. Plant Physiol. 2006, 141: 851-857. 10.1104/pp.106.081091." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3819">77</a>], they are likely regulated via nuclear cross-talk that is possibly more active in <i>N. sylvestris</i> than in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM14">14</a>).</p><p>In species of the <i>Nicotiana</i> genus, the conversion of nicotine to nornicotine, which is the precursor of the tobacco nitrosamine N'-nitrosonornicotine, is mediated by nicotine N-demethylase enzymes (NND) encoded by the CYP82E subfamily of cytochrome P450 genes. Four genes from this gene family are reported to be distributed in the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genomes. CYP82E4 is the dominant factor in senescence-inducible nornicotine production, whereas CYP82E5v2 is involved in nicotine conversion in the green leaves; both of them are found in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, along with CYP82E3. In <i>N. sylvestris</i>, one such gene, <i>CYP82E2</i>, has been found [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Gavilano LB, Siminszky B: Isolation and characterization of the cytochrome P450 gene CYP82E5v2 that mediates nicotine to nornicotine conversion in the green leaves of tobacco. Plant Cell Physiol. 2007, 48: 1567-1574. 10.1093/pcp/pcm128." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3854">78</a>]. Searches in both these <i>Nicotiana</i> genomes revealed that <i>N. sylvestris</i> has five related genes, and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> has four. A comparison of the phylogenetic trees (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM16">16</a>) confirms that three of the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genes are related to the <i>N. tabacum</i> <i>CYP82E3</i>, <i>CYP82E4</i> and <i>CYP82E5</i> genes and that one of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> genes corresponds to <i>N. tabacum</i> <i>CYP82E10</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Lewis RS, Bowen SW, Keogh MR, Dewey RE: Three nicotine demethylase genes mediate nornicotine biosynthesis in Nicotiana tabacum L.: Functional characterization of the CYP82E10 gene. Phytochemistry. 2010, 71: 1988-1998. 10.1016/j.phytochem.2010.09.011." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3895">79</a>]. The data presented in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM14">14</a> and Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM16">16</a> show that <i>NtomNND-1</i> is evolutionarily close to one copy of <i>CYP82E4</i>and highly expressed in flowers, whereas its expression in leaves is not supported by Affymetrix data. To our knowledge, the high expression of a nicotine demethylase gene in flowers has not yet been described; the gene product possibly plays a role in protection against insects. Conversely, the <i>NsylNND-1</i> that is evolutionary close to the <i>N. tabacum</i> <i>CYP82E10</i> is highly expressed in roots, confirming the findings of an earlier study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Lewis RS, Bowen SW, Keogh MR, Dewey RE: Three nicotine demethylase genes mediate nornicotine biosynthesis in Nicotiana tabacum L.: Functional characterization of the CYP82E10 gene. Phytochemistry. 2010, 71: 1988-1998. 10.1016/j.phytochem.2010.09.011." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3920">79</a>]. The high expression of the three <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genes related to the <i>N. tabacum</i> <i>CYP82E3</i>, <i>CYP82E4</i> and <i>CYP82E5</i> genes suggests that <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> is globally a more active producer of nornicotine than <i>N. sylvestris</i>, which is the opposite of what was found for nicotine synthesis (see above).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>Draft genomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> were assembled from Illumina short reads; the assemblies cover 83.3% and 71.7% of the calculated genome sizes (2.68 Gb and 2.36 Gb), respectively. Both assemblies have an N50 size of about 80 kb. The repeat content was determined to be 72 to 75% with a higher proportion of retrotransposons and copia-like LTRs in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> compared with <i>N</i>. <i>sylvestris</i>. The reported draft genomes offer good coverage of coding regions, as exemplified by the heavy-metal transport and alkaloid metabolism analyses. The examination of the terpenoid metabolism gene families is more challenging because their members are numerous and highly similar, and will require further investigations.</p><p>Tobacco SSR markers were mapped to both assemblies and a 65% concordance with PCR amplification data reported previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bindler G, Plieske J, Bakaher N, Gunduz I, Ivanov N, Van der Hoeven R, Ganal M, Donini P: A high density genetic map of tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum L.) obtained from large scale microsatellite marker development. Theor Appl Genet. 2011, 123: 219-230. 10.1007/s00122-011-1578-8." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d53014e3972">13</a>] was obtained. In addition, 5 to 7% of the markers that amplified in only one of the species could actually be mapped in both. Of the markers on the <i>N. acuminata</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genetic maps, 74 to 78% could be mapped to the genome assemblies.</p><p>The COSII markers from these two genetic maps were also mapped to both assemblies. In this case, only 31 to 34% of them could be mapped onto the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> assemblies, although when the same method was applied on the tomato genome, 84% of the markers present on the tomato genetic map could be mapped. This discrepancy could be due either to the still relatively high fragmentation of the <i>Nicotiana</i> genome assemblies, or to the COSII PCR primers not being suitable for the <i>Nicotiana</i> species.</p><p>The transcriptome assemblies revealed the expression of 44,000 to 53,000 transcripts in roots, leaves or flowers. Flowers had the most expressed transcripts, with about 3,500 expressed transcripts not detectable in roots or leaves. The merged species transcriptomes yielded 66,000 to 68,000 expressed transcripts, encoding 39,000 proteins. When these transcripts were clustered with genes from tomato and <i>Arabidopsis</i>, a core set of about 7,100 clusters, a Solanaceae-specific set of about 2,800 clusters, and a <i>Nicotiana</i>-specific set of about 3,600 clusters were identified.</p><p>Phenotypic differences observed between <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> could be explained by investigating the number of genes for specific protein families of the three metabolic pathways and their expressions in root, leaf and flower.</p><p>The SOL100 initiative aims to sequence a wide range of Solanaceae species to deepen our understanding of this plant family and improve breeding of its cultivars. The draft genomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> represent a significant contribution to this effort. Both are the ancestral species of allotetraploid tobacco (<i>N. tabacum</i>) with a 4.5 Gb genome, which currently represents a formidable challenge due to its high complexity. The genomes of the ancestor species provide a significant advance towards the assembly of the <i>N. tabacum</i> genome and illustrate a general strategy for the genomes of other polyploidy species such as wheat and cotton. These new genomes will increase the value of the already existing Solanaceae resources by providing additional comparative information at the genome and transcriptome levels and will help improve our understanding of plant metabolism and evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Illumina sequencing</h3><p>Young leaves, roots and flowers of <i>N. sylvestris</i> (USNGC TW136, PI555569) and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (USNGC TW142, PI555572) grown in a greenhouse were collected. DNA extraction was performed using Qiagen DNAeasy Plant Maxi Kit (Qiagen, Dsseldorf, Germany) from fresh leaves. RNA extraction was performed using the Qiagen RNAeasy Mini Kit (Qiagen).</p><p>Short insert 'paired-end' libraries were prepared using the Illumina TruSeq DNA Sample Preparation Kit version 2 according to the manufacturer's instructions, or with few modifications if prepared by Fasteris. For Fasteris, 2.1 mg of genomic DNA was broken using BioRuptor (Diagenode, Lige, Belgium); ends were repaired using Klenow and polynucleotide kinase, and then Fasteris-modified adapters were ligated to the inserts. After size selection on agarose gel, the libraries were amplified by ten PCR cycles, and then purified and quantified.</p><p>Long insert 'mate-pair' libraries were prepared using the Illumina Mate Pair Library Prep Kit version 2 according to the manufacturer's instructions, or using a Fasteris-developed protocol in which 10 mg of genomic DNA were broken into fragments of approximately 2 to 5 kb using Covaris (KBioSciences, Herts, UK) and purified on 0.7% agarose gel to recover fragments of 3 kb and 5 kb. After end repair, a Fasteris-designed spacer was ligated and the fragments were circularized. Non-circular fragments were eliminated and then the DNA was broken using Covaris to generate fragments of 400 bp, which were end repaired, ligated with Illumina adapters, purified on agarose gel and amplified by PCR for 12 cycles.</p><p>RNA-seq libraries were constructed using Illumina's TruSeq RNA Sample prep Kit protocol according to the manufacturer's instructions. All the libraries (short insert 'paired-end', long insert 'mate-pair', and RNA-seq) were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq-2000 using version 3 chemistry and flow-cells with runs of 2  100 bases. Base calling and sample demultiplexing were performed using Illumina's HiSeq Control Software and the CASAVA pipeline. The data for the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> RNA-seq triplicates have been uploaded to the EBI Sequence Read Archive under accession numbers ERP002501 and ERP002502, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome size estimation</h3><p>We estimated the genome size of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> using the 31-mer depth distribution of all the non-overlapping paired-end libraries, as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu X, Pan S, Cheng S, Zhang B, Mu D, Ni P, Zhang G, Yang S, Li R, Wang J: Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature. 2011, 475: 189-10.1038/nature10158." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4075">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Huang S, Li R, Zhang Z, Li L, Gu X, Fan W, Lucas WJ, Wang X, Xie B, Ni P: The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat Genet. 2009, 41: 1275-1281. 10.1038/ng.475." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4078">50</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Varshney RK, Chen W, Li Y, Bharti AK, Saxena RK, Schlueter JA, Donoghue MT, Azam S, Fan G, Whaley AM, Farmer AD, Sheridan J, Iwata A, Tuteja R, Penmetsa RV, Wu W, Upadhyaya HD, Yang SP, Shah T, Saxena KB, Michael T, McCombie WR, Yang B, Zhang G, Yang H, Wang J, Spillane C, Cook DR, May GD, Xu X, Jackson SA: Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 30: 83-89. 10.1038/nbt.2022." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4081">52</a>]. Briefly, the genome size is obtained by dividing the total number of 31-mers considered to be error-free by their most frequent depth of coverage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome assembly</h3><p>The raw DNA reads from <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> were preprocessed by first trimming 3' bases with qualities lower than 30, and then discarding reads shorter than 50 bases or with less than 90% of the bases with qualities lower than 30. The paired-end libraries with insert sizes shorter than 200 bases were further preprocessed using FLASH [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Mago T, Salzberg SL: FLASH: fast length adjustment of short reads to improve genome assemblies. Bioinformatics. 2011, 27: 2957-2963. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btr507." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4098">80</a>] to merge the paired-end reads into extended single reads.</p><p>The paired and single reads from the paired-end libraries were then assembled into contigs using SOAPdenovo [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Li R, Zhu H, Ruan J, Qian W, Fang X, Shi Z, Li Y, Li S, Shan G, Kristiansen K: De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010, 20: 265-272. 10.1101/gr.097261.109." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4104">81</a>] with a k-mer of 63, and the paired reads from paired-end and mate-pair libraries were used for scaffolding by increasing library size. To improve scaffolding, mate-pair libraries from closely related <i>Nicotiana</i> species were also used. Gaps that resulted from the scaffolding were closed using GapCloser and all sequences shorter than 200 bases were discarded from the final assemblies.</p><p>Superscaffolding using the tobacco WGP physical map was possible because it is based on sequencing tags, and the origin of the WGP contigs have been annotated. Briefly, WGP tags of S or T origin were mapped to the <i>N. sylvestris</i> or <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> sequences, respectively. Superscaffolds were created when two or more sequences could be anchored and oriented unambiguously to a WGP contig. The <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assemblies have been submitted to GenBank BioProjects PRJNA<u>182500</u> and PRJNA<u>182501</u>, respectively. The <i>N. sylvestris</i> whole genome shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession ASAF00000000. The version described in this paper is version ASAF01000000. The <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> whole genome shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession ASAG00000000. The version described in this paper is version ASAG01000000. The raw sequencing data used for the assemblies of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genomes have been submitted to the EBI Sequence Read Archive under accession numbers ERP002501 and ERP002502.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Repeat content estimation</h3><p>The repeat content of the <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome assemblies were estimated using RepeatMasker [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Smit A, Hubley R, Green P: RepeatMasker Open-3.0. [&#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4158">82</a>] with the eudicot repeat library available from the Sol Genomics Network, the TIGR Solanaceae repeat library, and RepeatScout [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Price AL, Jones NC, Pevzner PA: De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics. 2005, 21: i351-i358. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bti1018." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4161">83</a>] libraries created using sequences of at least 200 kb from the draft genome assemblies of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>. Classification of the repeat types was done using the NCBI BLASTN [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215: 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4171">84</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Camacho C, Coulouris G, Avagyan V, Ma N, Papadopoulos J, Bealer K, Madden TL: BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics. 2009, 10: 421-10.1186/1471-2105-10-421." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4174">86</a>] hits to known repeat elements.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genetic markers</h3><p>PCR primers for the SSR markers have been reported previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bindler G, Plieske J, Bakaher N, Gunduz I, Ivanov N, Van der Hoeven R, Ganal M, Donini P: A high density genetic map of tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum L.) obtained from large scale microsatellite marker development. Theor Appl Genet. 2011, 123: 219-230. 10.1007/s00122-011-1578-8." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4186">13</a>] and the COSII makers from Sol Genomics Network were mapped to the draft assembly genomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> using LAST [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Frith MC, Hamada M, Horton P: Parameters for accurate genome alignment. BMC Bioinformatics. 2010, 11: 80-10.1186/1471-2105-11-80." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4195">87</a>]. Only the primer pairs that could be mapped with at least 95% identity and that yielded a unique PCR product were retained.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Pathway gene identification and quantification</h3><p>Genomic regions containing genes that potentially encode proteins from the selected pathways were identified by mapping homologous proteins from other species to the genome assemblies using BLAT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Kent WJ: BLAT - the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 2002, 12: 656-664." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4206">88</a>] and manually curating the hits. Probes from the Tobacco Exon Array [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Martin F, Bovet L, Cordier A, Stanke M, Gunduz I, Peitsch MC, Ivanov NV: Design of a Tobacco Exon Array with application to investigate the differential cadmium accumulation property in two tobacco varieties. BMC Genomics. 2012, 13: 674-10.1186/1471-2164-13-674." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4209">49</a>] were selected by mapping them to the identified genome regions using LAST [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Frith MC, Hamada M, Horton P: Parameters for accurate genome alignment. BMC Bioinformatics. 2010, 11: 80-10.1186/1471-2105-11-80." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4212">87</a>] and retaining only perfect matches that could be mapped uniquely. Quantification of gene expression was obtained by summing the Cufflinks [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Roberts A, Pimentel H, Trapnell C, Pachter L: Identification of novel transcripts in annotated genomes using RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 2011, 27: 2325-2329. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btr355." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4215">89</a>] FPKM values of the transcripts that overlapped the identified genome regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20"><i>De novo</i> transcriptome assembly</h3><p>All the reads were preprocessed to clip the overrepresented sequences reported by FastQC [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="FastQC. [&#xA;                    http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4229">90</a>]. After clipping, the 3' ends of the reads were quality trimmed with a quality threshold of 20 and artifacts were removed. Finally, reads of at least 50 nucleotides with at least 75% nucleotides of quality 20 or more were kept. The clipping, trimming and filtering were performed using the fastx toolkit [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="FASTX-Toolkit. [&#xA;                    http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/index.html&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4232">91</a>]. Transcripts were assembled using the Trinity <i>de novo</i> assembly pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, Chen Z, Mauceli E, Hacohen N, Gnirke A, Rhind N, di Palma F, Birren BW, Nusbaum C, Lindblad-Toh K, Friedman N, Regev A: Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 29: 644-652. 10.1038/nbt.1883." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4238">92</a>]; the peptide prediction program contained within this software suite was used to predict peptides from the assembled transcripts.</p><p>Transcriptome assembly was performed using the 'Tuxedo' suite of tools. Reads were mapped to the appropriate genome assembly using the Bowtie2/Tophat2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Langmead B, Salzberg SL: Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 2012, 9: 357-359. 10.1038/nmeth.1923." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4244">93</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Trapnell C, Pachter L, Salzberg SL: TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 2009, 25: 1105-1111. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btp120." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4247">94</a>] pipeline with the default parameters. Transcript generation was performed using the Cufflinks tools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Roberts A, Pimentel H, Trapnell C, Pachter L: Identification of novel transcripts in annotated genomes using RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 2011, 27: 2325-2329. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btr355." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4250">89</a>] and merged using Cuffmerge. A representative set of transcript sequences was generated using the 'gtf_to_fasta' component of Cufflinks.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Transcript and protein quality</h3><p>The ORF finding utility included in the Trinity software package (version January 2012) was used to find ORFs in the inferred transcripts. Candidate peptide sequences were culled at a minimum length of 100 amino acids. The search for sequences homologous to the ORFs was performed using BLAST [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215: 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4261">84</a>], with the UniProt Knowledgebase and the Swiss-Prot subset as reference databases. A reasonably stringent e-value cutoff of 1E-30 was used and only one hit was retained for each sequence. To determine which of the best hits were mutual, a reverse search (reference database against transcripts) was also performed using the same parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Functional comparison to other species</h3><p>Orthologous and paralogous genes between our sequences and those from other species were clustered using OrthoMCL [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Chen F, Mackey AJ, Vermunt JK, Roos DS: Assessing performance of orthology detection strategies applied to eukaryotic genomes. PLoS One. 2007, 2: e383-10.1371/journal.pone.0000383." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4272">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ, Roos DS: OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003, 13: 2178-2189. 10.1101/gr.1224503." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4275">55</a>]. To ensure comparability, we used the same ORF finding software on the <i>Arabidopsis</i> (TAIR10 sequence) and tomato sequences (ITAG version 2.3) to derive peptide sequences and then used only sequences of 100 amino acids or longer. An all-against-all sequence search was performed using BLAST [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215: 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4281">84</a>] (version 2.2.23+) with default parameters and the results of this search were used as the input to OrthoMCL, which was run using the default parameter set (percentMatchCutoff = 50; e-valueExponentCutoff = -5). The OrthoMCL protein group output files were further processed using in-house Python scripts, and visualized in R [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Team RDC: R: A language and environment for statistical computing. 2008, Foundation Statistical Computing" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4284">95</a>] as a Venn diagram using the CRAN package 'VennDiagram' [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Chen H, Boutros PC: VennDiagram: a package for the generation of highly-customizable Venn and Euler diagrams in R. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011, 12: 35-10.1186/1471-2105-12-35." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4288">96</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Functional annotation</h3><p>The EFICAz<sup>2.5</sup> software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Kumar N, Skolnick J: EFICAz2. 5: Application of a high-precision enzyme function predictor to 396 proteomes. Bioinformatics. 2012, 28: 2687-2688. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bts510." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4301">97</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Tian W, Arakaki AK, Skolnick J: EFICAz: a comprehensive approach for accurate genome-scale enzyme function inference. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004, 32: 6226-6239. 10.1093/nar/gkh956." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4304">99</a>] was used to predict EC numbers for the protein sequences predicted from the transcripts of the pooled tissue samples. The InterProScan software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Zdobnov EM, Apweiler R: InterProScan - an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics. 2001, 17: 847-848. 10.1093/bioinformatics/17.9.847." href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d53014e4307">100</a>], version 4.8 was used to assign GO terms to the protein sequences.</p></div></div></section>

The objective of this work was to provide a contribution to the SOL100 initiative and was not for the development of tobacco-based products.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Authors' contributions</h3><p>NS, NVI and MCP conceived and designed the study. SG and LB provided plant material and contributed to the biological interpretation of the results. NB provided the genetic maps of <i>Nicotiana sylvestris</i> and <i>Nicotiana tomentosiformis</i>. SO prepared the libraries and performed the Illumina sequencing. NS and JB carried out genome and transcriptome analysis. NS, JB, LB, and NVI wrote the manuscript. NVI supervised the study. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec24" data-title="Electronic supplementary material"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec24-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec24">Electronic supplementary material</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec24-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div>

The <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> proteins are numbered according to the rows of <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/gb-2013-14-6-r60#MOESM14">Additional file 14</a>.
